lang="en"><i>Voyager 1</i></h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" style="display:none">Space probe and the farthest human-made object from Earth</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width:25em;"><caption><i>Voyager 1980</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Distance</th><td>6,490&#160;km (4,030&#160;mi)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">Flagship</a></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Voyager 1</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> on September 5, 1977. Part of the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager program</a> to study the outer <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, <i>Voyager 1</i> was launched 16 days after its twin, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>. Having operated for 41&#160;years, 5&#160;months and 12&#160;days as of February 17, 2019, the spacecraft still communicates with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> to receive routine commands and to transmit data to Earth. At a distance of 145.11 astronomical units (2.1708<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;km; 1.3489<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;mi) (21.708 billion kilometers; 13.489 billion miles) from Earth as of February 16, 2019,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-3">[3]</a></sup> it is the most distant human-made object from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probe\'s objectives included flybys of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and Saturn\'s largest moon, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. While the spacecraft\'s course could have been altered to include a <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> encounter by forgoing the Titan flyby, exploration of the moon, which was known to have a substantial atmosphere, took priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-faq-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-SD-7">[7]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 1</i> studied the weather, magnetic fields, and rings of the two planets and was the first probe to provide detailed images of their <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>.\n</p><p>After completing its primary mission with the flyby of Saturn on November 12, 1980, <i>Voyager 1</i> became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_leaving_the_Solar_System" title="List of artificial objects leaving the Solar System">third of five artificial objects</a> to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> required to <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Farthest_regions" title="Solar System">leave the Solar System</a>. On August 25, 2012, <i>Voyager 1</i> became the first spacecraft to cross the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a> and enter the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130912_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130912-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a further testament to the robustness of <i>Voyager 1</i>, the Voyager team completed a successful test of the spacecraft\'s <i>trajectory correction maneuver</i> (TCM) thrusters in late 2017 (the first time these thrusters were fired since 1980), a project enabling the mission to be extended by two to three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backup_thrusters_test_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backup_thrusters_test-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s extended mission is expected to continue until about 2025 when its <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> will no longer supply enough electric power to operate its scientific instruments.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_background">Mission background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1960s, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_Grand_Tour" title="Planetary Grand Tour">Grand Tour</a> to study the outer planets was proposed which prompted NASA to begin work on a mission in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.1960_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.1960-10">[10]</a></sup> Information gathered by the <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> spacecraft helped <i>Voyager</i>\'s engineers design <i>Voyager</i> to cope more effectively with the intense radiation environment around Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rad_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-rad-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initially, <i>Voyager 1</i> was planned as "<i>Mariner 11</i>" of the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a>. Due to budget cuts, the mission was scaled back to be a flyby of Jupiter and Saturn and renamed the Mariner Jupiter-Saturn probes. As the program progressed, the name was later changed to Voyager, since the probe designs began to differ greatly from previous Mariner missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goo.win_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-goo.win-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_components">Spacecraft components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spacecraft components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program#Spacecraft_design" title="Voyager program">Voyager program &#167;&#160;Spacecraft design</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1027" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png/160px-Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png/240px-Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png/320px-Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_Program_-_High-gain_antenna_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 3.7&#160;m (12&#160;ft)  diameter <a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">high gain dish antenna</a> used on the <i>Voyager</i> craft</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Voyager 1</i> was constructed by the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_CNN_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_CNN-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trailblazer_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trailblazer-15">[15]</a></sup> It has 16 <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> thrusters, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-axis_stabilization" title="Three-axis stabilization">three-axis stabilization</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyroscopes" title="Gyroscopes">gyroscopes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">referencing instruments</a> to keep the probe\'s radio antenna pointed toward Earth. Collectively, these instruments are part of the Attitude and Articulation Control Subsystem (AACS), along with redundant units of most instruments and 8 backup thrusters. The spacecraft also included 11 scientific instruments to study celestial objects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a> as it travels through space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDS-Host_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDS-Host-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication_system">Communication system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Communication system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The radio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_system" title="Communication system">communication system</a> of <i>Voyager 1</i> was designed to be used up to and beyond the limits of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. The communication system includes a 3.7-meter (12&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a>  high gain <a href="/wiki/Cassegrain_antenna" title="Cassegrain antenna">Cassegrain antenna</a> to send and receive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a> via the three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> stations on the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.hga_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.hga-17">[17]</a></sup> The craft normally transmits data to Earth over Deep Space Network Channel 18, using a frequency of either 2.3&#160;GHz or 8.4&#160;GHz, while signals from Earth to <i>Voyager</i> are transmitted at 2.1&#160;GHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.tele_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.tele-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <i>Voyager 1</i> is unable to communicate directly with the Earth, its digital <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_storage" title="Magnetic storage">tape</a> recorder (DTR) can record about 64 kilobytes of data for transmission at another time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.77.136_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.77.136-19">[19]</a></sup> Signals from <i>Voyager 1</i> take over 19 hours to reach Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><i>Voyager 1</i> has three <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> (RTGs) mounted on a boom. Each <a href="/wiki/MHW-RTG" title="MHW-RTG">MHW-RTG</a> contains 24 pressed <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a> oxide spheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NuclearNews_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NuclearNews-20">[20]</a></sup> The RTGs generated about 470 W of <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> at the time of launch, with the remainder being dissipated as waste heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.life_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.life-21">[21]</a></sup> The power output of the RTGs declines over time (due to the 87.7-year <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of the fuel and degradation of the thermocouples), but the craft\'s RTGs will continue to support some of its operations until 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDS-Host_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDS-Host-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NuclearNews_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-NuclearNews-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png/154px-Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png/231px-Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png/308px-Voyager_Program_-_RTG_diagram_1.png 2x" width="154"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Diagram of RTG fuel container, showing the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a> oxide spheres\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:20px auto;"><a class="image" class="gallerytext">\n<p>Diagram of RTG shell, showing the power-producing <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>-<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> <a href="/wiki/Thermocouple" title="Thermocouple">thermocouples</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Voyager_Program_-_RTG_upclose.png/255px-Voyager_Program_-_RTG_upclose.png 2x" width="127"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Model of an RTG unit\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>As of February 17, 2019, <i>Voyager 1</i> has 72.06% of the plutonium-238 that it had at launch. By 2050, it will have 56.5% left.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computers">Computers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Computers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Unlike the other onboard instruments, the operation of the cameras for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> is not autonomous, but rather it is controlled by an imaging parameter table contained in one of the on-board <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_computer" title="Digital computer">digital computers</a>, the Flight Data Subsystem (FDS). Since the 1990s, most space probes have had completely <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">autonomous</a> cameras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The computer command subsystem (CCS) controls the cameras. The CCS contains fixed computer programs, such as command decoding, fault-detection and -correction routines, antenna pointing routines, and spacecraft sequencing routines. This computer is an improved version of the one that was used in the 1970s <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking orbiters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.ch6-2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.ch6-2-23">[23]</a></sup> The hardware in both custom-built CCS subsystems in the <i>Voyager</i>s is identical. There is only a minor software modification: one of them that has a scientific subsystem that the other lacks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The Attitude and Articulation Control Subsystem (AACS) controls the spacecraft orientation (its <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude</a>). It keeps the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a> pointing towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Earth" title="The Earth">the Earth</a>, controls attitude changes, and points the scan platform. The custom-built AACS systems on both <i>Voyager</i>s are the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_instruments">Scientific instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scientific instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager program</a></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col" style="width:135px;">Instrument name\n</th>\n<th scope="col" style="width:50px;"><abbr style="background:#8300b5;">\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Blue\n</td>\n<td>430&#8211;530&#160;nm\n</td>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_-_Filters_-_NaD.png"><img alt="Voyager - Filters - NaD.png" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="25" style="background:#ffe200;">\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Orange\n</td>\n<td>590&#8211;640&#160;nm\n</td>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" style="background:#ff8900;">\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">CH<sub>4</sub></a>-JST\n</td>\n<td>614&#8211;624&#160;nm\n</td>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_-_Filters_-_CH4JST.png"><img alt="Voyager - Filters - CH4JST.png" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="25" style="background:#ff7b00;">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> Bradford Smith / University of Arizona (PDS/PRN website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PDI data catalog, PDS/PRN data catalog</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-partial" style="background:#FFB;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Radio Science System <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(disabled)</span>\n</td>\n<td>RSS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Utilized the telecommunications system of the Voyager spacecraft to determine the physical properties of planets and satellites (ionospheres, atmospheres, masses, gravity fields, densities) and the amount and size distribution of material in Saturn\'s rings and the ring dimensions. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-02" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> G. Tyler / Stanford University PDS/PRN overview</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog, PDS/PRN data catalog (VG_2803), NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-partial" style="background:#FFB;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Infrared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferometer" title="Interferometer">Interferometer</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</a> <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(disabled)</span>\n</td>\n<td>IRIS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Investigates both global and local energy balance and atmospheric composition. Vertical temperature profiles are also obtained from the planets and satellites as well as the composition, thermal properties, and size of particles in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_rings" title="Saturn\'s rings">Saturn\'s rings</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-03" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> Rudolf Hanel / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center (PDS/PRN website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PRN data catalog, PDS/PRN expanded data catalog (VGIRIS_0001, VGIRIS_002), NSSDC Jupiter data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-partial" style="background:#FFB;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</a> <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(disabled)</span>\n</td>\n<td>UVS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Designed to measure atmospheric properties, and to measure radiation. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-04" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> A. Broadfoot / University of Southern California (PDS/PRN website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PRN data catalog</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-yes" style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Triaxial Fluxgate <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(active)</span>\n</td>\n<td>MAG\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Designed to investigate the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> of Jupiter and Saturn, the interaction of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetospheres</a> of these planets, and the magnetic field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_space" title="Interplanetary space">interplanetary space</a> out to the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and the magnetic field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-05" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Norman_F._Ness" title="Norman F. Ness">Norman F. Ness</a> / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center (website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog, NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-no" style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">Plasma</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</a> <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(defective)</span>\n</td>\n<td>PLS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Investigates the microscopic properties of the plasma ions and measures electrons in the energy range from 5 eV to 1 keV. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-06" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> John Richardson / <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> (website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog, NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-yes" style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Low Energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charged_Particle" title="Charged Particle">Charged Particle</a> Instrument <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(active)</span>\n</td>\n<td>LECP\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Measures the differential in energy fluxes and angular distributions of ions, electrons and the differential in energy ion composition. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-07" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Stamatios_Krimigis" title="Stamatios Krimigis">Stamatios Krimigis</a> / <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">JHU</a> / APL / University of Maryland (JHU/APL website / UMD website / KU website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> UMD data plotting, PDS/PPI data catalog, NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-yes" style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Ray_System" title="Cosmic Ray System">Cosmic Ray System</a> <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(active)</span>\n</td>\n<td>CRS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Determines the origin and acceleration process, life history, and dynamic contribution of interstellar cosmic rays, the nucleosynthesis of elements in cosmic-ray sources, the behavior of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a>, and the trapped planetary energetic-particle environment. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-08" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Edward_C._Stone" title="Edward C. Stone">Edward Stone</a> / Caltech / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center (website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog, NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-partial" style="background:#FFB;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;">Planetary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Astronomy" title="Radio Astronomy">Radio Astronomy</a> Investigation <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(disabled)</span>\n</td>\n<td>PRA\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Utilizes a sweep-frequency radio receiver to study the radio-emission signals from Jupiter and Saturn. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-10" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> James Warwick / University of Colorado</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog, NSSDC data archive</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-no" style="background:#F99;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Polarimeter" title="Polarimeter">Photopolarimeter</a> System <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(defective)</span>\n</td>\n<td>PPS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Utilized a telescope with a <a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizer</a> to gather information on surface texture and composition of Jupiter and Saturn and information on atmospheric scattering properties and density for both planets. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-11" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> Arthur Lane / JPL (PDS/PRN website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PRN data catalog</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="table-yes" style="background:#9F9;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Waves_in_plasmas" title="Waves in plasmas">Plasma Wave</a> System <br/><span style="font-size:85%;">(active)</span>\n</td>\n<td>PWS\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;">Provides continuous, sheath-independent measurements of the electron-density profiles at Jupiter and Saturn as well as basic information on local wave&#8211;particle interaction, useful in studying the magnetospheres. <a class="external text" href="https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experimentDisplay.do?id=1977-084A-13" rel="nofollow">More</a>\n<div style="font-size:85%;"><ul><li><b>Principal investigator:</b> Donald Gurnett / University of Iowa (website)</li><li><b>Data:</b> PDS/PPI data catalog</li></ul></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i>For more details on the Voyager space probes\' identical instrument packages, see the separate article on the overall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voyager_Program#Spacecraft_design" title="Voyager Program">Voyager Program</a>.</i><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r874315073">.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output href="/wiki/File:Voyager_1_skypath_1977-2030.png"><img alt="Voyager 1 skypath 1977-2030.png" data-file-height="2298" data-file-width="6930" decoding="async" height="265" are tested in their first use since November 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_trajectory">Launch and trajectory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Launch and trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2469" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg/170px-Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg/255px-Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg/340px-Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan_3E_with_Voyager_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Voyager 1</i> lifted off atop a <a href="/wiki/Titan_IIIE" title="Titan IIIE">Titan IIIE</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif/220px-Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif/330px-Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif/440px-Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Voyager_1_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>Voyager 1</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from  September 1977 to 31 December 1981<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i>Voyager 1</i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: Cyan ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: Yellow ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> </span></div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Voyager 1</i> probe was launched on September 5, 1977, from Launch Complex 41 at the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a>, aboard a <a href="/wiki/Titan_IIIE" title="Titan IIIE">Titan IIIE</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carrier_rocket" title="Carrier rocket">launch vehicle</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> probe had been launched two weeks earlier, on August 20, 1977. Despite being launched later, <i>Voyager 1</i> reached both Jupiter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.jupiter_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.jupiter-29">[29]</a></sup> and Saturn sooner, following a shorter trajectory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.planetary_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.planetary-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1999" data-file-width="2455" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png/220px-Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png/330px-Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png/440px-Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager-1_Jupiter-flyby_March-5-1979.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The trajectory of <i>Voyager 1</i> through the Jupiter system</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flyby_of_Jupiter">Flyby of Jupiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flyby of Jupiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Jupiter" title="Exploration of Jupiter">Exploration of Jupiter</a></div>\n<p><i>Voyager 1</i> began photographing <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> in January 1979. Its closest approach to Jupiter was on March 5, 1979, at a distance of about 349,000 kilometers (217,000 miles) from the planet\'s center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.jupiter_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.jupiter-29">[29]</a></sup> Because of the greater photographic resolution allowed by a closer approach, most observations of the moons, rings, magnetic fields, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_belt" title="Radiation belt">radiation belt</a> environment of the Jovian system were made during the 48-hour period that bracketed the closest approach. <i>Voyager 1</i> finished photographing the Jovian system in April 1979.\n</p><p>Discovery of active volcanic activity on the moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> was probably the greatest surprise. It was the first time active volcanoes had been seen on another body in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It appears that activity on Io affects the entire <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jovian system</a>. Io appears to be the primary source of matter that pervades the Jovian magnetosphere &#8211; the region of space that surrounds the planet influenced by the planet\'s strong <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">Sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, apparently erupted by Io\'s volcanoes and sputtered off the surface by impact of high-energy particles, were detected at the outer edge of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Magnetosphere of Jupiter">magnetosphere of Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.jupiter_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.jupiter-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two Voyager space probes made a number of important discoveries about Jupiter, its satellites, its radiation belts, and its never-before-seen <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">planetary rings</a>.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n<li class="gallerybox" class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/18px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> Media related to <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Photos_of_Jupiter_system_by_Voyager_1" title="commons:Category:Photos of Jupiter system by Voyager 1">the <i>Voyager 1</i> Jupiter encounter</a> at Wikimedia Commons</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flyby_of_Saturn">Flyby of Saturn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Flyby of Saturn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Saturn" title="Exploration of Saturn">Exploration of Saturn</a></div>\n<p>The gravitational assist trajectories at Jupiter were successfully carried out by both Voyagers, and the two spacecraft went on to visit <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and its system of moons and rings. <i>Voyager 1</i> encountered Saturn in November 1980, with the closest approach on November 12, 1980, when the space probe came within 124,000 kilometers (77,000&#160;mi) of Saturn\'s cloud-tops. The space probe\'s cameras detected complex structures in the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> instruments studied the atmospheres of Saturn and its giant moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.saturn_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.saturn-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i> found that about seven percent of the volume of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>\'s upper atmosphere is <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (compared with 11 percent of Jupiter\'s atmosphere), while almost all the rest is <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. Since Saturn\'s internal helium abundance was expected to be the same as Jupiter\'s and the Sun\'s, the lower abundance of helium in the upper atmosphere may imply that the heavier helium may be slowly sinking through Saturn\'s hydrogen; that might explain the excess heat that Saturn radiates over energy it receives from the Sun. Winds blow at high speeds in <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. Near the equator, the Voyagers measured winds about 500&#160;m/s (1,100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Miles_per_hour" title="Miles per hour">mph</a>). The wind blows mostly in an easterly direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.planetary_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.planetary-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Voyagers found <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> emissions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> at mid-latitudes in the atmosphere, and auroras at polar latitudes (above 65 degrees). The high-level auroral activity may lead to the formation of complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbon molecules</a> that are carried toward the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. The mid-latitude auroras, which occur only in sunlit regions, remain a puzzle, since bombardment by electrons and ions, known to cause auroras on Earth, occurs primarily at high latitudes. Both Voyagers measured the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">rotation of Saturn</a> (the length of a day) at 10 hours, 39 minutes, 24 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.saturn_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.saturn-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i>\'s mission included a flyby of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, Saturn\'s largest moon, which had long been known to have an atmosphere. Images taken by <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i> in 1979 had indicated the atmosphere was substantial and complex, further increasing interest. The Titan flyby occurred as the spacecraft entered the system to avoid any possibility of damage closer to Saturn compromising observations, and approached to within 6,400&#160;km (4,000&#160;mi), passing behind Titan as seen from Earth and the Sun. Voyager\'s measurement of the atmosphere\'s effect on sunlight and Earth-based measurement of its effect on the probe\'s radio signal were used to determine the atmosphere\'s composition, density, and pressure. Titan\'s mass was also measured by observing its effect on the probe\'s trajectory. The thick haze prevented any visual observation of the surface, but the measurement of the atmosphere\'s composition, temperature, and pressure led to speculation that lakes of liquid hydrocarbons could exist on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell2015_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell2015-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because observations of Titan were considered vital, the trajectory chosen for <i>Voyager 1</i> was designed around the optimum Titan flyby, which took it below the south pole of Saturn and out of the plane of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>, ending its planetary science mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swift1997_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swift1997-33">[33]</a></sup> Had <i>Voyager 1</i> failed or been unable to observe Titan, <i>Voyager 2</i>\'s trajectory would have been altered to incorporate the Titan flyby,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell2015_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bell2015-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>94</span></sup> precluding any visit to Uranus and Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-faq-5">[5]</a></sup> The trajectory <i>Voyager 1</i> was launched into would not have allowed it to continue on to Uranus and Neptune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swift1997_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swift1997-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>155</span></sup> but could have been altered to avoid a Titan flyby and travel from Saturn to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, arriving in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-SD-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275.33333333333px"><div style="width: 275.33333333333px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 273.33333333333px;"><div class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Family_portrait_(Voyager_1).png"><img alt="A set of grey squares trace roughly left to right. (Voyager)">Family Portrait</a></i> of the Solar System acquired by <i>Voyager 1</i></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png/220px-Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png/440px-Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_1_-_14_February_1990.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Position of <i>Voyager 1</i> above the plane of the ecliptic on February 14, 1990</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="659" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/220px-Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/330px-Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/440px-Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Voyager 1</i> and <i>2</i> speed and distance from Sun</div></div></div>\n<p>On February 14, 1990, <i>Voyager 1</i> took the first ever "<a href="/wiki/Family_Portrait_(Voyager)" title="Family Portrait (Voyager)">family portrait</a>" of the Solar System as seen from outside,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.family_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.family-34">[34]</a></sup> which includes the image of planet Earth known as <i><a href="/wiki/Pale_Blue_Dot" title="Pale Blue Dot">Pale Blue Dot</a></i>. Soon afterwards its cameras were deactivated to conserve energy and computer resources for other equipment. The camera software has been removed from the spacecraft, so it would now be complex to get them working again. Earth-side software and computers for reading the images are also no longer available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-faq-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pale_Blue_Dot.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/200px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/300px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/400px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pale_Blue_Dot.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Pale_Blue_Dot" title="Pale Blue Dot">Pale Blue Dot</a></i> image showing Earth from 6&#160;billion kilometers appearing as a tiny dot (the blueish-white speck approximately halfway down the brown band to the right) within the darkness of deep space</div></div></div>\n<p>On February 17, 1998, <i>Voyager 1</i> reached a distance of 69 AU from the Sun and overtook <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> as the most distant spacecraft from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn.9802_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn.9802-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g.2013sep13_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-g.2013sep13-36">[36]</a></sup> Travelling at about 17 kilometers per second (11&#160;mi/s)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goo.50_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-goo.50-37">[37]</a></sup> it has the fastest heliocentric <a href="/wiki/Recessional_velocity" title="Recessional velocity">recession speed</a> of any spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dd.fast_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-dd.fast-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As <i>Voyager 1</i> headed for interstellar space, its instruments continued to study the Solar System. Jet Propulsion Laboratory scientists used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_wave" title="Plasma wave">plasma wave</a> experiments aboard <i>Voyager 1</i> and <i>2</i> to look for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a>, the boundary at which the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> transitions into the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.int_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.int-39">[39]</a></sup> As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the probe was moving with a relative velocity to the Sun of about 17,030 meters per second (55,900&#160;ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.201309062_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.201309062-40">[40]</a></sup>\nWith the velocity the probe is currently maintaining, <i>Voyager 1</i> is traveling about 325&#160;million miles (523<span style="margin-left:0.2em">&#215;<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;km) per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> or approximately half a <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-year</a> per ten millennia.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Termination_shock">Termination shock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Termination shock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_Path.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="794" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Voyager_Path.svg/200px-Voyager_Path.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Voyager_Path.svg/300px-Voyager_Path.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Voyager_Path.svg/400px-Voyager_Path.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_Path.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close flybys of gas giants gave <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assists</a> to both Voyagers</div></div></div>\n<p>Scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> believe that <i>Voyager 1</i> entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Termination_shock" title="Termination shock">termination shock</a> in February 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn.20031105_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn.20031105-42">[42]</a></sup> This marks the point where the solar wind slows down to subsonic speeds. Some other scientists expressed doubt, discussed in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> of November 6, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n.426021_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-n.426021-43">[43]</a></sup> The issue would not be resolved until other data became available, since <i>Voyager 1\'</i>s solar-wind detector ceased functioning in 1990. This failure meant that termination shock detection would have to be inferred from the data from the other instruments on board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n.02068_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-n.02068-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n.02066_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-n.02066-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grl.018291_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-grl.018291-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2005, a NASA press release said that the consensus was that <i>Voyager 1</i> was then in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliosheath" title="Heliosheath">heliosheath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frontier_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-frontier-47">[47]</a></sup> In a scientific session at the <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a> meeting in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> on May 25, 2005, Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ed_Stone" title="Ed Stone">Ed Stone</a> presented evidence that the craft crossed the termination shock in late 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cross_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-cross-48">[48]</a></sup> This event is estimated to have occurred on December 15, 2004 at a distance of 94 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cross_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-cross-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.timeline_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.timeline-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heliosheath">Heliosheath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Heliosheath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On March 31, 2006, <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">amateur radio operators</a> from <a href="/wiki/AMSAT" title="AMSAT">AMSAT</a> in Germany tracked and received radio waves from <i>Voyager 1</i> using the 20-meter (66&#160;ft) dish at <a href="/wiki/Bochum" title="Bochum">Bochum</a> with a long integration technique. Retrieved data was checked and verified against data from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> station at Madrid, Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ham_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ham-50">[50]</a></sup> This seems to be the first such amateur tracking of <i>Voyager 1</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ham_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-ham-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was confirmed on December 13, 2010 that <i>Voyager 1</i> had passed the reach of the radial outward flow of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, as measured by the Low Energy Charged Particle device. It is suspected that solar wind at this distance turns sideways because of interstellar wind pushing against the heliosphere. Since June 2010, detection of solar wind had been consistently at zero, providing conclusive evidence of the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.36121_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.36121-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis2011_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis2011-52">[52]</a></sup> On this date, the spacecraft was approximately 116 AU or 10.8&#160;billion miles (17.3&#160;billion kilometers) from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.11988466_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.11988466-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i> was commanded to change its orientation to measure the sideways motion of the solar wind at that location in space on March 2011 (~33yr 6mo from launch). A test roll done in February had confirmed the spacecraft\'s ability to maneuver and reorient itself. The course of the spacecraft was not changed. It rotated 70 degrees counterclockwise with respect to Earth to detect the solar wind. This was the first time the spacecraft had done any major maneuvering since the <a href="/wiki/Family_Portrait_(Voyager)" title="Family Portrait (Voyager)"><i>Family Portrait</i> photograph</a> of the planets was taken in 1990. After the first roll the spacecraft had no problem in reorienting itself with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a>, <i>Voyager 1</i>\'s guide star, and it resumed sending transmissions back to Earth. <i>Voyager 1</i> was expected to enter interstellar space "at any time". <i>Voyager 2</i> was still detecting outward flow of solar wind at that point but it was estimated that in the following months or years it would experience the same conditions as <i>Voyager 1</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.mission_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.mission-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.12688246_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.12688246-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft was reported at 12.44&#176; declination and 17.163 hours right ascension, and at an ecliptic latitude of 34.9&#176; (the ecliptic latitude changes very slowly), placing it in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Ophiuchus" title="Ophiuchus">Ophiuchus</a> as observed from the Earth on May 21, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faq_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-faq-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 1, 2011, it was announced that <i>Voyager 1</i> had detected the first <a href="/wiki/Lyman_series" title="Lyman series">Lyman-alpha radiation</a> originating from the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy. Lyman-alpha radiation had previously been detected from other galaxies, but because of interference from the Sun, the radiation from the Milky Way was not detectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ng.111201_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ng.111201-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA announced on December 5, 2011, that <i>Voyager 1</i> had entered a new region referred to as a "cosmic purgatory". Within this stagnation region, charged particles streaming from the Sun slow and turn inward, and the Solar System\'s magnetic field is doubled in strength as interstellar space appears to be applying pressure. Energetic particles originating in the Solar System decline by nearly half, while the detection of high-energy electrons from outside increases 100-fold. The inner edge of the stagnation region is located approximately 113 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn.20111206_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn.20111206-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heliopause">Heliopause</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Heliopause">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:220px;max-width:220px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png/218px-Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png/327px-Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png/436px-Cosmic_Rays_at_Voyager_1.png 2x" width="218"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Plot showing a dramatic increase in the rate of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> particle detection by the <i>Voyager 1</i> spacecraft (October 2011 through October 2012)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:220px;max-width:220px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png/218px-Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png/327px-Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png/436px-Solar_wind_at_Voyager_1.png 2x" width="218"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Plot showing a dramatic decrease in the rate of <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> particle detection by <i>Voyager 1</i> (October 2011 through October 2012)</div></div></div></div>\n<p>NASA announced in June 2012 that the probe was detecting changes in the environment that were suspected to correlate with arrival at the heliopause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.16167_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.16167-58">[58]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 1</i> had reported a marked increase in its detection of charged particles from interstellar space, which are normally deflected by the solar winds within the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a> from the Sun. The craft thus began to enter the interstellar medium at the edge of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.20120614_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.20120614-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i> became the first spacecraft to cross the heliopause in August 2012, then at a distance of 121 AU from the Sun, although this was not confirmed for another year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2013_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2013-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghose2013_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghose2013-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen2013_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowen2013-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr2013_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr2013-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gurnett2013_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gurnett2013-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of September 2012, sunlight took 16.89 hours to get to <i>Voyager 1</i> which was at a distance of 121 AU. The <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of the Sun from the spacecraft was &#8722;16.3 (less than 30 times the brightness of the full moon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-65">[65]</a></sup> The spacecraft was traveling at 17.043&#160;km/s (10.590&#160;mi/s) relative to the Sun. It would need about 17,565 years at this speed to travel a <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-65">[65]</a></sup> To compare, <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, the closest star to the Sun, is about 4.2 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016396434357355000&#9824;</span>2.65<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>5</sup>&#160;AU</span>) distant. Were the spacecraft traveling in the direction of that star, 73,775 years would pass before <i>Voyager 1</i> reaches it. (<i>Voyager 1</i> is heading in the direction of the constellation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophiuchus_(constellation)" title="Ophiuchus (constellation)">Ophiuchus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-65">[65]</a></sup>)\n</p><p>In late 2012, researchers reported that particle data from the spacecraft suggested that the probe had passed through the heliopause. Measurements from the spacecraft revealed a steady rise since May in collisions with high energy particles (above 70 MeV), which are thought to be <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> emanating from <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions far beyond the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, with a sharp increase in these collisions in late August. At the same time, in late August, there was a dramatic drop in collisions with low-energy particles, which are thought to originate from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lifeslittlemysteries.com_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-lifeslittlemysteries.com-66">[66]</a></sup> Ed Roelof, space scientist at Johns Hopkins University and principal investigator for the Low-Energy Charged Particle instrument on the spacecraft declared that "Most scientists involved with Voyager 1 would agree that [these two criteria] have been sufficiently satisfied."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lifeslittlemysteries.com_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-lifeslittlemysteries.com-66">[66]</a></sup> However, the last criterion for officially declaring that <i>Voyager 1</i> had crossed the boundary, the expected change in magnetic field direction (from that of the Sun to that of the interstellar field beyond), had not been observed (the field had changed direction by only 2 degrees<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghose2013_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ghose2013-61">[61]</a></sup>), which suggested to some that the nature of the edge of the heliosphere had been misjudged. On December 3, 2012, <i>Voyager</i> project scientist Ed Stone of the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a> said, "<i>Voyager</i> has discovered a new region of the heliosphere that we had not realized was there. We\'re still inside, apparently. But the magnetic field now is connected to the outside. So it\'s like a highway letting particles in and out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam.20121204_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam.20121204-67">[67]</a></sup> The magnetic field in this region was 10 times more intense than <i>Voyager 1</i> encountered before the termination shock. It was expected to be the last barrier before the spacecraft exited the Solar System completely and entered interstellar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d.121203_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-d.121203-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam.20121203_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam.20121203-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyNews2012.12_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-DailyNews2012.12-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2013, it was announced that <i>Voyager 1</i> might have become the first spacecraft to enter interstellar space, having detected a marked change in the plasma environment on August 25, 2012. However, until September 12, 2013, it was still an open question as to whether the new region was interstellar space or an unknown region of the Solar System. At that time, the former alternative was officially confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agu_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-agu-71">[71]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_We_Know_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_We_Know-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013 <i>Voyager 1</i> was exiting the Solar System at a speed of about 3.6 AU per year, while <i>Voyager 2</i> is going slower, leaving the Solar System at 3.3 AU per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Each year <i>Voyager 1</i> increases its lead over <i>Voyager 2</i>.\n</p><p><i>Voyager 1</i> reached a distance of 135 AU from the Sun on May 18, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-3">[3]</a></sup> By September 5, 2017, that had increased to about 139.64 AU from the Sun, or just over 19 light-hours, and at that time <i>Voyager 2</i> was 115.32 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Its progress can be monitored at NASA\'s website (see: External links).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interstellar_probes_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1322" data-file-width="2005" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg/350px-Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg/525px-Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg/700px-Interstellar_probes_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interstellar_probes_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Voyager 1</i> and the other probes that are in or on their way to interstellar space, except <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>.</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_medium">Interstellar medium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Voyager_1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Interstellar medium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;"
